{"code":1,"isDone":false,"toInsertArticleList":[{"createdTime":"2020-10-17 00:44:33","updatedTime":"2020-10-17 00:44:33","title":"New ‘Rick and Morty’ Loot Crate Brings the Existential Crisis of the Butter Robot to Your Dinner Table","link":"https://www.slashfilm.com/?p=641551","description":"<p><img class=\"size-medium wp-image-641559 aligncenter\" src=\"https://www.slashfilm.com/wp/wp-content/images/rickandmorty-butterrobot-crisis-700x305.jpg\" alt=\"New Rick and Morty Loot Crate\" width=\"700\" height=\"305\" srcset=\"https://www.slashfilm.com/wp/wp-content/images/rickandmorty-butterrobot-crisis-700x305.jpg 700w, https://www.slashfilm.com/wp/wp-content/images/rickandmorty-butterrobot-crisis-360x157.jpg 360w, https://www.slashfilm.com/wp/wp-content/images/rickandmorty-butterrobot-crisis-768x335.jpg 768w, https://www.slashfilm.com/wp/wp-content/images/rickandmorty-butterrobot-crisis.jpg 941w\" sizes=\"(max-width: 700px) 100vw, 700px\" /></p>\n<p>The fourth season of <strong><em>Rick and Morty</em></strong> has come and gone, and it&#8217;s already <a href=\"https://www.amazon.com/dp/B08DBYMT2T/?tag=film-20\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener\">available on Blu-ray and DVD</a>. While fans are eagerly anticipating the show&#8217;s return for season five (whenever that may be), Loot Crate will attempt to hold them over with a new box of exclusive<em> Rick and Morty</em> gear and collectibles to kick off 2021.</p>\n<p>A new <em>Rick and Morty</em> Loot Crate is going on sale starting tonight, and the theme is &#8220;DYS-FUNCTION,&#8221; as it is with almost every episode of the Adult Swim animated series. There will be a bunch of <em>Rick and Morty</em>-related items in the box, but perhaps the most satisfying is a replica of the butter robot from &#8220;Something Ricked This Way Comes,&#8221; a throwback to the ninth episode of the very first season. Check it out below.<span id=\"more-641551\"></span></p>\n<h2>New Rick and Morty Loot Crate Preview</h2>\n\n<a href='https://www.slashfilm.com/new-rick-and-morty-loot-crate-january-2021/rickandmorty-lootcrate-butterrobot-photo1/'><img width=\"360\" height=\"425\" src=\"https://www.slashfilm.com/wp/wp-content/images/rickandmorty-lootcrate-butterrobot-photo1-360x425.jpg\" class=\"attachment-thumbnail size-thumbnail\" alt=\"New Rick and Morty Loot Crate - Butter Robot\" srcset=\"https://www.slashfilm.com/wp/wp-content/images/rickandmorty-lootcrate-butterrobot-photo1-360x425.jpg 360w, https://www.slashfilm.com/wp/wp-content/images/rickandmorty-lootcrate-butterrobot-photo1.jpg 700w\" sizes=\"(max-width: 360px) 100vw, 360px\" /></a>\n<a href='https://www.slashfilm.com/new-rick-and-morty-loot-crate-january-2021/rickandmorty-lootcrate-butterrobot-photo2/'><img width=\"360\" height=\"425\" src=\"https://www.slashfilm.com/wp/wp-content/images/rickandmorty-lootcrate-butterrobot-photo2-360x425.jpg\" class=\"attachment-thumbnail size-thumbnail\" alt=\"New Rick and Morty Loot Crate - Butter Robot\" srcset=\"https://www.slashfilm.com/wp/wp-content/images/rickandmorty-lootcrate-butterrobot-photo2-360x425.jpg 360w, https://www.slashfilm.com/wp/wp-content/images/rickandmorty-lootcrate-butterrobot-photo2.jpg 700w\" sizes=\"(max-width: 360px) 100vw, 360px\" /></a>\n\n<p>Even though it&#8217;s not a fully mechanized version of the butter robot that was depressingly built simply for passing butter, this butter dish designed to look exactly like the robot in question is still a delightful little item. Let the butter robot&#8217;s existential crisis remind you that no matter how bad life gets, at least your sole purpose isn&#8217;t serving butter. Maybe you can even put it out at Thanksgiving, let your family ask you what this butter dish is all about, show them an episode of <em>Rick and Morty</em>, and have your grandparents be disappointed in you. Then you&#8217;ll have a family just like Rick and Morty.</p>\n<p><iframe width=\"740\" height=\"416\" src=\"https://www.youtube.com/embed/X7HmltUWXgs?feature=oembed\" frameborder=\"0\" allow=\"accelerometer; autoplay; clipboard-write; encrypted-media; gyroscope; picture-in-picture\" allowfullscreen></iframe></p>\n<p>The rest of the stuff in the new <em>Rick and Morty</em> Loot Crate includes items &#8220;that are especially suited to help Jerry (and you) work through any problems you may be having.&#8221; Loot Crate isn&#8217;t revealing everything in the box since some of the items are supposed to be a surprise, but we know two of the other items that will be included.</p>\n<p><img class=\"size-medium wp-image-641560 aligncenter\" src=\"https://www.slashfilm.com/wp/wp-content/images/rickandmorty-toitletpaper-jerrysocks-700x394.jpg\" alt=\"New Rick and Morty Loot Crate\" width=\"700\" height=\"394\" srcset=\"https://www.slashfilm.com/wp/wp-content/images/rickandmorty-toitletpaper-jerrysocks-700x394.jpg 700w, https://www.slashfilm.com/wp/wp-content/images/rickandmorty-toitletpaper-jerrysocks-360x203.jpg 360w, https://www.slashfilm.com/wp/wp-content/images/rickandmorty-toitletpaper-jerrysocks-768x432.jpg 768w, https://www.slashfilm.com/wp/wp-content/images/rickandmorty-toitletpaper-jerrysocks.jpg 1000w\" sizes=\"(max-width: 700px) 100vw, 700px\" /></p>\n<p>One item is a roll of toilet paper with drawings inspired by &#8220;The Old Man and the Seat,&#8221; including the King of Shit version of Rick. The other is a pair of Jerry&#8217;s sock puppet therapy socks from the fourth season finale, &#8220;<span class=\"aCOpRe\">Star Mort Rickturn of the Jerri.&#8221;</span> And in case you forget what they&#8217;re for, they&#8217;re labeled.</p>\n<p>Again, these aren&#8217;t the only items in the new <em>Rick and Morty</em> Loot Crate, but if you want to see the rest, you&#8217;ll have to pick one up by ordering. You can <a href=\"https://www.lootcrate.com/crates/rick-and-morty\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener\">pre-order one for $39.99</a> starting tonight just after 12:00 A.M. ET / 9:00 P.M. PT when the previous quarter&#8217;s Loot Crate is changed over to the new one. It will be on sale until January 15 at 9:00 P.M. PT, and will ship shortly thereafter.</p>\n<p>The post <a rel=\"nofollow\" href=\"https://www.slashfilm.com/new-rick-and-morty-loot-crate-january-2021/\">New &#8216;Rick and Morty&#8217; Loot Crate +0000","feedId":21,"bgimg":"https://www.slashfilm.com/wp/wp-content/images/rickandmorty-butterrobot-crisis-700x305.jpg","linkMd5":"e73f51ddb3714cd917c981cf87ad66e4","bgimgJsdelivr":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx2/cdn10@2020_3/2020/10/16/16-44-33-587_066ce72e1a3813c3.webp","destWidth":700,"destHeight":305,"sourceBytes":28524,"destBytes":20242,"author":"Ethan Anderton","articleImgCdnMap":{"https://www.slashfilm.com/wp/wp-content/images/rickandmorty-butterrobot-crisis-700x305.jpg":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx2/cdn10@2020_3/2020/10/16/16-44-33-587_066ce72e1a3813c3.webp","https://www.slashfilm.com/wp/wp-content/images/rickandmorty-lootcrate-butterrobot-photo1-360x425.jpg":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx2/cdn52@2020_6/2020/10/16/16-45-04-473_b319f424fc58da58.webp","https://www.slashfilm.com/wp/wp-content/images/rickandmorty-lootcrate-butterrobot-photo2-360x425.jpg":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx23/cdn74@2020_1/2020/10/16/16-45-03-390_d0a9f2c58d221b9c.webp","https://www.slashfilm.com/wp/wp-content/images/rickandmorty-toitletpaper-jerrysocks-700x394.jpg":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx25/cdn24@2020_2/2020/10/16/16-45-03-506_6ae4cd81b800f9a3.webp","http://feeds.feedburner.com/~ff/slashfilm?d=yIl2AUoC8zA":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx23/cdn88@2020_1/2020/10/16/16-45-04-423_9a442e8760d02cd4.webp","http://feeds.feedburner.com/~ff/slashfilm?i=Opzv0tZ7WZ4:WgssolHFJ5s:V_sGLiPBpWU":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx7/cdn20@2020_2/2020/10/16/16-45-04-360_f4f0558e8d4d1cfe.webp","http://feeds.feedburner.com/~ff/slashfilm?d=I9og5sOYxJI":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx23/cdn100@2020_1/2020/10/16/16-45-05-292_879c5505f034c3d2.webp","http://feeds.feedburner.com/~ff/slashfilm?i=Opzv0tZ7WZ4:WgssolHFJ5s:EGzC5_Ws_ZE":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx18/cdn20@2020_4/2020/10/16/16-45-03-380_a9f996d82afb7ee8.webp","http://feeds.feedburner.com/~r/slashfilm/~4/Opzv0tZ7WZ4":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx/cdn32@2020_1/2020/10/16/16-45-03-215_dd4f0fdecf29b860.webp"},"publishedOrCreatedDate":1602866673063},{"createdTime":"2020-10-17 00:44:33","updatedTime":"2020-10-17 00:44:33","title":"‘Honeydew’ Review: An Unforgettable Horror Movie Score Helps Serve Up One Uniquely Sinister Experience [Nightstream]","link":"https://www.slashfilm.com/?p=641441","description":"<p><img class=\"size-medium wp-image-641458 aligncenter\" src=\"https://www.slashfilm.com/wp/wp-content/images/Honeydew-Review-700x300.jpg\" alt=\"Honeydew Review\" width=\"700\" height=\"300\" srcset=\"https://www.slashfilm.com/wp/wp-content/images/Honeydew-Review.jpg 700w, https://www.slashfilm.com/wp/wp-content/images/Honeydew-Review-360x154.jpg 360w\" sizes=\"(max-width: 700px) 100vw, 700px\" /></p>\n<p><span style=\"font-weight: 400;\">Science and religion unite in an unconditional manner with writer/director </span><b>Devereux Milburn</b><span style=\"font-weight: 400;\">’</span><span style=\"font-weight: 400;\">s </span><strong><i>Honeydew</i></strong><span style=\"font-weight: 400;\">. Making its world premiere at Nightstream Festival, </span><i><span style=\"font-weight: 400;\">Honeydew</span></i><span style=\"font-weight: 400;\"> is as auditorily savory as it is visually sadistic. A blend of horror sub-genres, </span><span style=\"font-weight: 400;\">Milburn</span><span style=\"font-weight: 400;\"> proves that he has a talent (and an appetite) for serving audiences a disturbing new take at an exploration in the countryside gone wrong.</span></p>\n<p><span id=\"more-641441\"></span></p>\n<p><span style=\"font-weight: 400;\">Rylie (</span><b>Malin Barr</b><span style=\"font-weight: 400;\">) is a PhD student who is doing her thesis on a specific parasite that has invaded crops in a certain region. As a result, large amounts of cattle in the area died off and several people experienced physical ailments from ingesting the grain. While researching the countryside on a road trip with her boyfriend Sam (</span><b>Sawyer Spielberg</b><span style=\"font-weight: 400;\">), the two end up accidentally trespassing on a piece of property. Having to pack up and find another place to stay for the night, they encounter a small house in the woods owned by an old woman named Karen (</span><b>Barbara Kingsley</b><span style=\"font-weight: 400;\">). She attempts to help Rylie and Sam by calling a neighbor to fix their car, but he never shows. Karen cooks a late dinner for the couple and leads them down into her basement so they can stay the night in a room tucked far away from sunlight. Once they start getting ready for bed, hallucinations start to kick in and their evening quickly spins into an uncontrollable nightmare.</span></p>\n<p><span style=\"font-weight: 400;\">There are several familiar elements at play in </span><i><span style=\"font-weight: 400;\">Honeydew</span></i><span style=\"font-weight: 400;\">. The storyline is reminiscent of </span><b><i>Hansel &#38; Gretel</i></b><span style=\"font-weight: 400;\">, </span><b><i>The Hills Have Eyes</i></b><span style=\"font-weight: 400;\">, and </span><b><i>The Texas Chain Saw Massacre</i></b><span style=\"font-weight: 400;\"> at various points. Rylie and Sam have a strained relationship from the beginning and get easily frustrated with one another. Their chemistry is subtly toxic and creates a tension on camera before they even enter Karen</span><span style=\"font-weight: 400;\">’</span><span style=\"font-weight: 400;\">s house. Once they meet the old woman, the film grows increasingly distressing but is disguised as hospitality through Karen</span><span style=\"font-weight: 400;\">’</span><span style=\"font-weight: 400;\">s kind demeanor. Aloof yet friendly, her avoidance on certain topics and insistence on them staying becomes awkward and demanding. With nowhere else to go, the couple complies. </span></p>\n<p><span style=\"font-weight: 400;\">What sets </span><i><span style=\"font-weight: 400;\">Honeydew</span></i><span style=\"font-weight: 400;\"> apart from the films and sub-genre it is reminiscent of is its incredible sound design. Composer </span><b>John Mehrmann</b><span style=\"font-weight: 400;\"> utilizes organic sounds in order to create a menacing atmosphere that is unlike any other. While many horror films use string instruments to induce terror or silence to induce madness and fear, Mehrmann utilizes an interesting combination of sounds that are almost like a symphony of foley. Sounds of a sharp object being slid across metal, popping or bubbling noises people would normally make with their mouth, and heavy rhythmic breaths all accumulate in various ways to create dread in a deceptively welcoming environment. </span></p>\n<p><span style=\"font-weight: 400;\">At times, the film</span><span style=\"font-weight: 400;\">’</span><span style=\"font-weight: 400;\">s composition is harrowing and abrasive. For example, echoing and loud chanting occurs as if a demonic choir</span><span style=\"font-weight: 400;\">’</span><span style=\"font-weight: 400;\">s singing is rising straight up from the depths of Hell. Other moments have a soundscape that is light-hearted and boppy, even during scenes that are particularly stress-inducing. The music complements the characters as they both tease the overall tone of the film. Just when viewers think they can let their guard down for a minute, the sound design and odd characters suggest otherwise. </span></p>\n<p><span style=\"font-weight: 400;\">The unique audio is coupled with visual split screens that contribute to Sam and Riley</span><span style=\"font-weight: 400;\">’</span><span style=\"font-weight: 400;\">s hallucinations and distorted sense of time and place. The use of multiple scenes being shown at once provides a collective horror that is heartbreaking and deeply uncomfortable. It asks the question: what is scarier and sadder, to be in a dangerous situation alone or with the one you love but there may not be anything you can do about it?</span></p>\n<p><span style=\"font-weight: 400;\">Kingsley</span> <span style=\"font-weight: 400;\">is an engaging terror as Karen. While she is able to be sweet and inviting by humming Christmas songs over a warm stovetop, it is the reason </span><i><span style=\"font-weight: 400;\">why</span></i><span style=\"font-weight: 400;\"> she is cooking and </span><i><span style=\"font-weight: 400;\">why</span></i><span style=\"font-weight: 400;\"> she is so happy to have company that makes her kindness turn sinister. Karen</span><span style=\"font-weight: 400;\">’</span><span style=\"font-weight: 400;\">s religious devotion is an answer to everything that ails her and her community after the plight of their crop. Her religious dedication is delusional and foils Rylie</span><span style=\"font-weight: 400;\">’</span><span style=\"font-weight: 400;\">s scientific and analytical mind. Barr</span> <span style=\"font-weight: 400;\">does a fantastic job at subduing her emotions in order to appease Sam, who is eager to dismiss continuous red flags. Spielberg</span> <span style=\"font-weight: 400;\">successfully delivers an engaging and complex character as Sam. While he bickers with Rylie, Sam also has a distinctive relationship with food that is normally attributed to female characters on screen. There is an element of body image from a male perspective and health concerns which prohibit him from indulging in items like sugar, salt, and carbs. So, when Karen offers him to help himself to any leftovers, Sam sneaks away in the middle of the night and ends up eating with Karen</span><span style=\"font-weight: 400;\">’</span><span style=\"font-weight: 400;\">s eccentric and mute son. This plays into one of the film</span><span style=\"font-weight: 400;\">’</span><span style=\"font-weight: 400;\">s themes of deprivation and indulgence which is interesting to view through a male character.</span></p>\n<p><i><span style=\"font-weight: 400;\">Honeydew </span></i><span style=\"font-weight: 400;\">is a nightmarish exploration of religious perversion and the dangers associated with what we consume both physically and mentally. Mehrmann</span><span style=\"font-weight: 400;\">’</span><span style=\"font-weight: 400;\">s sound design and composition is a refreshingly rare addition to the horror genre and allows for the tone of the film to freely fluctuate under a veil of perpetual danger. Milburn</span><span style=\"font-weight: 400;\">’</span><span style=\"font-weight: 400;\">s directorial debut is sinister, shocking, and unlike its title, </span><i><span style=\"font-weight: 400;\">far</span></i><span style=\"font-weight: 400;\"> from sweet. </span></p>\n<p><strong>/Film Rating: 7.5 out of 10</strong></p>\n<p>The post <a rel=\"nofollow\" href=\"https://www.slashfilm.com/honeydew-review/\">&#8216;Honeydew&#8217; Review: An Unforgettable Horror Movie Score Helps Serve Up One Uniquely Sinister Experience [Nightstream]</a> appeared first 00:44:33","updatedTime":"2020-10-17 00:44:33","title":"‘Fried Green Tomatoes’ TV Series From NBC and Norman Lear Will Star Reba McEntire","link":"https://www.slashfilm.com/?p=641495","description":"<p><a href=\"https://www.slashfilm.com/wp/wp-content/images/fried-green-tomatoes-tv-series.jpg\"><img class=\"aligncenter size-medium wp-image-641513\" src=\"https://www.slashfilm.com/wp/wp-content/images/fried-green-tomatoes-tv-series-700x321.jpg\" alt=\"fried green tomatoes tv series\" width=\"700\" height=\"321\" srcset=\"https://www.slashfilm.com/wp/wp-content/images/fried-green-tomatoes-tv-series-700x321.jpg 700w, https://www.slashfilm.com/wp/wp-content/images/fried-green-tomatoes-tv-series-360x165.jpg 360w, https://www.slashfilm.com/wp/wp-content/images/fried-green-tomatoes-tv-series-768x352.jpg 768w, https://www.slashfilm.com/wp/wp-content/images/fried-green-tomatoes-tv-series.jpg 948w\" sizes=\"(max-width: 700px) 100vw, 700px\" /></a></p>\n<p><strong><em>Fried Green Tomatoes</em></strong>, Fannie Flagg&#8217;s 1987 novel that became a 1991 Jon Avnet film, is now headed to TV. The legendary<strong> Norman Lear</strong> is executive producing the series for NBC, and <strong>Reba McEntire</strong> will star, with <strong>Jennifer Cecil</strong> attached to write and executive produce. The series is described as a &#8220;modernization of the novel and movie that explores the lives of descendants from the original work.&#8221;</p>\n<p><span id=\"more-641495\"></span></p>\n<p><a href=\"https://variety.com/2020/tv/news/fried-green-tomatoes-series-reba-mcentire-norman-lear-nbc-1234803671/\">Variety</a> has the scoop on the <em>Fried Green Tomatoes</em> TV series that will star Reba McEntire. Here&#8217;s how they&#8217;re describing the show:</p>\n<blockquote><p>The hour-long drama project is described as a modernization of the novel and movie that explores the lives of descendants from the original work. When present-day Idgie Threadgoode (McEntire) returns to Whistle Stop after a decade away, she must wrestle with a changed town, estranged daughter, faltering cafe and life-changing secret.</p></blockquote>\n<p>The novel – which was titled <em>Fried Green Tomatoes at the Whistle Stop Cafe</em> – followed two women: &#8220;Evelyn, who’s in the sad slump of middle age, and gray-headed Mrs. Threadgoode, who’s telling her life story. Her tale includes two more women—the irrepressibly daredevilish tomboy Idgie and her friend Ruth—who back in the thirties ran a little place in Whistle Stop, Alabama, offering good coffee, southern barbecue, and all kinds of love and laughter—even an occasional murder. And as the past unfolds, the present will never be quite the same again.&#8221;</p>\n<p>The book was adapted into a 1991 film that starred Kathy Bates, Jessica Tandy, Mary Stuart Masterson, Mary-Louise Parker, and Cicely Tyson. The film was a hit, and ended up nominated for two academy awards: Best Supporting Actress for Jessica Tandy, and Best Adapted Screenplay. However, the movie did take some liberties with the source material. In Flagg&#8217;s book, the characters of Idgie and Ruth have a romantic affair, but the movie skirts around that and keeps things a bit ambiguous. Here&#8217;s a <em>very</em> retro home video trailer for the film.</p>\n<p><iframe width=\"740\" height=\"555\" src=\"https://www.youtube.com/embed/o5hb5mc-dfI?feature=oembed\" frameborder=\"0\" allow=\"accelerometer; autoplay; clipboard-write; encrypted-media; gyroscope; picture-in-picture\" allowfullscreen></iframe></p>\n<p>McEntire will executive produce along with Norman Lear, a legend in the TV business whose credits include <em>All in the Family</em>, <em>The Jeffersons</em>, <em>Sanford and Son</em>, <em>One Day at a Time</em>, and many more. The series will also be executive produced by Jennifer Cecil, who will also write. Cecil&#8217;s credits include <em>Hell on Wheels</em>, <em>One Tree Hill</em>, <em>Private Practice</em>, <em>Brothers and Sisters</em>, <em>Notorious</em>, and more. Other executive producers on the series include <strong>Brent Miller</strong> and the book&#8217;s author, Fannie Flagg.</p>\n<p>The post <a rel=\"nofollow\" href=\"https://www.slashfilm.com/fried-green-tomatoes-tv-series/\">&#8216;Fried Green Tomatoes&#8217; TV Series From NBC and Norman Lear Will Star Reba McEntire</a> appeared first on <a joined the <strong><em>Doctor Strange and the Multiverse of Madness</em></strong><em> </em>cast. Who is she playing? We have no idea! Like the Multiverse of Madness itself, this is a story shrouded in mystery. But it&#8217;s just another sign that Marvel is soldiering on with their productions even as the pandemic leaves the future of movie theaters – and, by extension, big movies – up in the air.</p>\n<p><span id=\"more-641641\"></span></p>\n<p>Deadline broke the news about Xochitl Gomez joining the <em>Doctor Strange 2</em> cast, adding &#8220;Plot details and what character Gomez will be playing are unknown at this time.&#8221; Which means I don&#8217;t have a whole lot to tell you here, folks! Here&#8217;s what we know: <strong>Benedict Cumberbatch</strong> is back as Doctor Steven Strange, a neurosurgeon turned Sorcerer Supreme. <strong>Sam Raimi</strong> is directing, which is kind of a big deal – it&#8217;s his first superhero movie since <em>Spider-Man 3</em>. And speaking of <em>Spider-Man</em>, Cumberbatch is <em>also</em> playing Strange in the <em>Spider-Man: Far From Home</em> sequel, with rumors swirling that he&#8217;ll be taking over the mentor role for Tom Holland&#8217;s Peter Parker. Cumberbatch will film his <em>Spider-Man</em> scenes first and then get to work on <em>Doctor Strange and the Multiverse of Madness</em>. Per Deadline, <em>Doctor Strange</em> is hoping to start production in May 2021.</p>\n<p>Original <em>Doctor Strange</em> director Scott Derickson was set to helm the sequel but departed over creative differences. He&#8217;ll remain an executive producer on the project. <em>Doctor Strange</em> actors <strong>Benedict Wong</strong> and <strong>Chiwetel Ejiofor</strong> are both expected to return for the film, and <strong>Elizabeth Olsen</strong> will also appear as Scarlet Witch, with <em>Multiverse of Madness</em> expected to coincide with the Disney+ series <em>WandaVision</em>.</p>\n<p>The film&#8217;s title suggests that the MCU will finally open up the Multiverse, a series of alternate universes that have similar characters in different situations. The Multiverse was teased in <em>Spider-Man: Far From Home</em>, but ended up all being a rouse concocted by the film&#8217;s villain, Mysterio. <em>Spider-Man: Into the Spider-Verse</em>, which <em>isn&#8217;t</em> an MCU film, played around with a Multiverse angle as well.</p>\n<p>Having not watched a single second of Netflix&#8217;s <em>The Baby-Sitters Club</em>, I can&#8217;t tell you much about Xochitl Gomez. She&#8217;s 14-years-old, so I can&#8217;t even really speculate on which character she might be playing in the film. As of now, <em>Doctor Strange and the Multiverse of Madness</em> is due to arrive in theaters on <strong>March 25, 2022</strong>, but these days, movie release dates are not set in stone.</p>\n<p>The 00:44:33","updatedTime":"2020-10-17 00:44:33","title":"‘The Mandalorian’ Season 3 Will (Probably) Start Filming This Year","link":"https://www.slashfilm.com/?p=641530","description":"<p><a href=\"https://www.slashfilm.com/wp/wp-content/images/mandalorian-season-3-filming.jpg\"><img class=\"aligncenter size-medium wp-image-641554\" src=\"https://www.slashfilm.com/wp/wp-content/images/mandalorian-season-3-filming-700x321.jpg\" alt=\"mandalorian season 3 filming\" width=\"700\" height=\"321\" srcset=\"https://www.slashfilm.com/wp/wp-content/images/mandalorian-season-3-filming-700x321.jpg 700w, https://www.slashfilm.com/wp/wp-content/images/mandalorian-season-3-filming-360x165.jpg 360w, https://www.slashfilm.com/wp/wp-content/images/mandalorian-season-3-filming-768x352.jpg 768w, https://www.slashfilm.com/wp/wp-content/images/mandalorian-season-3-filming.jpg 948w\" sizes=\"(max-width: 700px) 100vw, 700px\" /></a></p>\n<p><strong><em>The Mandalorian</em></strong> season 2 isn&#8217;t even out yet, but <strong>Jon Favreau</strong> isn&#8217;t wasting any time. The <strong><em>Star Wars</em></strong> TV series creator says that if all goes according to plan, <em>The Mandalorian</em> season 3 filming should commence before the end of 2020. Unlike many other shows, <em>The Mandalorian</em>&#8216;s StageCraft technology – a gigantic digital video screen that allows filmmakers to &#8220;capture a significant amount of complex visual effects shots in-camera using real-time game engine technology and LED screens to represent dynamic photo-real digital landscapes and sets with creative flexibility previously unimaginable&#8221; – keeps it a relatively small affair, production-wise. This will no doubt help the show avoid running into problems with potential COVID-19 scenarios.</p>\n<p><span id=\"more-641530\"></span></p>\n<p>While speaking with <a href=\"https://variety.com/2020/tv/news/star-wars-mandalorian-movie-pedro-pascal-jon-favreau-1234805417/\">Variety</a>, Jon Favreau, creator of <em>The Mandalorian</em>, revealed that season 3 of the hit Disney+ would probably start shooting before the end of this year. &#8220;We&#8217;re operating under the assumption that we’ll be able to go forward,&#8221; Favreau said, adding: &#8220;We&#8217;re in very small situations and oftentimes we have a lot of characters in masks. And we also have a lot of digital work that augments things. So we’re a show that’s probably well-equipped to be flexible based on the protocols that are emerging surrounding work restarting.&#8221;</p>\n<p>While other movie and TV productions are having to find ways to work around the ongoing coronavirus pandemic,<em> The Mandalorian</em> employs ILM&#8217;s StageCraft technology, which puts it in a unique position. As ILM <a href=\"https://www.ilm.com/hatsrabbits/virtual-production-on-the-mandalorian/\">explains</a>:</p>\n<blockquote><p>Over 50 percent of <em>The Mandalorian</em> Season One was filmed using this ground-breaking new methodology, eliminating the need for location shoots entirely. Instead, actors in<em> The Mandalorian</em> performed in an immersive and massive 20’ high by 270-degree semicircular LED video wall and ceiling with a 75’-diameter performance space, where the practical set pieces were combined with digital extensions on the screens. Digital 3D environments created by ILM played back interactively on the LED walls, edited in real-time during the shoot&#8230;</p></blockquote>\n<p>Elsewhere in the same interview, Favreau was asked if there will ever be a <em>Mandalorian</em> movie. Favreau said he&#8217;s in &#8220;no rush&#8221; to do so, but also added that he was &#8220;definitely open to it, and excited to see where the story leads us and have that flexibility — because there’s no rulebook now.&#8221; In other words, to quote <em>The Simpsons</em>, Short answer: Yes, with an &#8220;if.&#8221; Long answer: No, with a &#8220;but.&#8221;</p>\n<p><em>The Mandalorian</em> season 2 will see &#8220;The Mandalorian and the Child continue their journey, facing enemies and rallying allies as they make their way through a dangerous galaxy in the tumultuous era after the collapse of the Galactic Empire.&#8221; Look for it on Disney+ <strong>October 30</strong>.</p>\n<p>The post <a rel=\"nofollow\" Evangelista","articleImgCdnMap":{"https://www.slashfilm.com/wp/wp-content/images/mandalorian-season-3-filming-700x321.jpg":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx23/cdn36@2020_3/2020/10/16/16-44-33-386_86ed75c14c35176d.webp","http://feeds.feedburner.com/~ff/slashfilm?d=yIl2AUoC8zA":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx23/cdn88@2020_1/2020/10/16/16-45-04-423_9a442e8760d02cd4.webp","http://feeds.feedburner.com/~ff/slashfilm?i=t_KDv1kTexY:6rDNvT2uPnE:V_sGLiPBpWU":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx16/cdn48@2020_3/2020/10/16/16-45-04-356_ff19063573d6d82f.webp","http://feeds.feedburner.com/~ff/slashfilm?d=I9og5sOYxJI":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx23/cdn100@2020_1/2020/10/16/16-45-05-292_879c5505f034c3d2.webp","http://feeds.feedburner.com/~ff/slashfilm?i=t_KDv1kTexY:6rDNvT2uPnE:EGzC5_Ws_ZE":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx14/cdn16@2020_6/2020/10/16/16-45-04-416_2a6085c71ef5bf3e.webp","http://feeds.feedburner.com/~r/slashfilm/~4/t_KDv1kTexY":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx7/cdn96@2020_2/2020/10/16/16-45-03-283_2206aa7fc0dbbc35.webp"},"publishedOrCreatedDate":1602866673064},{"createdTime":"2020-10-17 00:44:33","updatedTime":"2020-10-17 00:44:33","title":"Clint Mansell on Composing the ‘Rebecca’ Score in the Shadow of Hitchcock, Incorporating “The Devil’s Instrument,” and More [Interview]","link":"https://www.slashfilm.com/?p=641411","description":"<p><img class=\"alignnone size-medium wp-image-641567 aligncenter\" src=\"https://www.slashfilm.com/wp/wp-content/images/Clint-Mansell-interview-700x300.jpg\" alt=\"Clint Mansell interview\" width=\"700\" height=\"300\" srcset=\"https://www.slashfilm.com/wp/wp-content/images/Clint-Mansell-interview.jpg 700w, https://www.slashfilm.com/wp/wp-content/images/Clint-Mansell-interview-360x154.jpg 360w\" sizes=\"(max-width: 700px) 100vw, 700px\" /></p>\n<p>For more than twenty years, <strong>Clint Mansell</strong> has created beautiful and artistically daring scores for a wide variety of films. His work on Darren Aronofsky&#8217;s <em>Requiem for a Dream</em> remains one of the most haunting scores of this millennium, and his other credits – from <em>Moon</em> to <em>Stoker</em> to <em>The Fountain</em> and <em>Black Swan</em> – represent a depth and sophistication that&#8217;s rare in an era in which many scores are little more than background noise.</p>\n<p>Mansell previously collaborated with director Ben Wheatley on <em>High-Rise</em>, and now he&#8217;s re-teamed with the filmmaker for <em><strong>Rebecca</strong></em>, a new adaptation of Daphne du Maurier&#8217;s gothic romance novel. His score is one of the best parts of the entire film: it captures the unease of Lily James&#8217; main character and builds a moody, occasionally eerie foundation for the mysteries of the story. We spoke with Mansell about creating new music in the shadow of Alfred Hitchcock&#8217;s Best Picture-winning 1940 adaptation, using &#8220;the devil&#8217;s instrument&#8221; as part of the score, how he infuses music with emotion, and more.</p>\n<p>We&#8217;re also happy to premiere a full track from Mansell&#8217;s <em>Rebecca</em> score, so immerse yourself in a spooky atmosphere while reading the interview by pressing play below.<span id=\"more-641411\"></span></p>\n<h2>&#8220;The Wings of Mercury&#8221;</h2>\n<p><iframe width=\"740\" height=\"416\" src=\"https://www.youtube.com/embed/gfepy2fXV-0?feature=oembed\" frameborder=\"0\" allow=\"accelerometer; autoplay; clipboard-write; encrypted-media; gyroscope; picture-in-picture\" allowfullscreen></iframe></p>\n<h2>Clint Mansell Interview</h2>\n<p class=\"p1\"><i>Note: this conversation has been edited for clarity and brevity.</i></p>\n<p class=\"p1\"><b>I read that you studied English literature in college. Had you read Daphne du Maurier’s book before you signed on to this project?</b></p>\n<p class=\"p1\">It wasn’t part of my curriculum back then, but I had read it some years ago. I can’t say that I totally re-read it this time, but I did [come close]. The shadow of Hitchcock’s version probably hangs heavier than the book really, just because I guess that’s how society – or at least my world – is these days. Literature, for a lot of people – myself included, and I do consider myself a reader – but in the modern world, it’s been supplanted really, by not just movies but by the pace of technology. So going back and re-investing in it definitely gives you a different sense of time and pace, particularly.</p>\n<p class=\"p1\">Also, peripherals, if that’s the right word. In a book, you get to know a lot more things that don’t make it into the movie: background, atmosphere, feelings are discussed more. From a composer’s point of view, that’s really helpful. I felt the same way when I did <i>High-Rise</i> with Ben. So much of the composer’s work, for me anyway, isn’t just what’s on the screen. You’re trying to bring in a whole atmosphere, a whole universe, if you like. That may hide in the words of the pages a bit more than the film…from a composer’s point of view, there’s almost another world that you can dig under the surface that may or may not be helpful to you when you’re creating the music for the film.</p>\n<p class=\"p1\"><b>Can you talk about your approach for crafting the score for this movie, in addition to re-investing in the book?</b></p>\n<p class=\"p1\">It felt like a very different film for both Ben and I, really. We discussed those realities: biggish studio films with lots of people having opinions and involvement, and what an aim of a movie like this is. I’ve composed work on a lot of stuff at the same time half the time, but I didn’t feel like I could do that: I just focused entirely on <i>Rebecca</i> whilst I was doing this and ended up being on it for over a year, part of that being because of being shut down by the pandemic. But that gave me a lot of time to research and to absorb the film.</p>\n<p class=\"p1\">I went back to something that I kind of stopped doing over the years. I did a 20 year <i>Requiem of a Dream</i> anniversary for the Toronto International Film Festival recently, and talking about that reminded me that I used to write on the script a lot, back in the early days of my formative years [as a composer]. It was something that I’d kind of moved away from over time, but I went back to that with <i>Rebecca</i>. I got the script pretty early, and he asked me to do the film before we shot it, so I had a lot of time to think about it and invest in it, you know? It’s a period piece, but it’s gotta be a modern film because people are watching it now. So you sort of have a foot in the past and a foot in the future. We sort of played around with, how far can we go toward the classic, melodramatic approach to old-fashioned filmmaking? And where would the line be where we go, “Oh, that’s too much. That starts to suck.” So I got into a lot of stuff from the script and just jamming on ideas that we could just play with and think about: “More of this, less of that.” Time is always one of my favorite things to have on a film. A good film to work on is another big part, but having time to absorb and think and invest in what you’re doing is always wonderful news.</p>\n<p class=\"p1\">The other thing I did was, on the Criterion version of [Hitchcock’s] <i>Rebecca</i>, <strong>Franz Waxman</strong>’s score is an isolated track, so you can just listen to the score and watch the film. That was a fantastic education. Not in the “Oh, I should do what he did” way, but the way that it works. I had written something like 40 to 50 pieces of just rough ideas – a minute long, three minutes long, ten seconds long – I had all these different material I’d written from reading the script and the book and thinking about things. I thought, “OK, if I was scoring this in 1939, I’d probably be following some form of template that went on in movies at that time,” and Waxman nailed that incredibly in that film. You start off with the opening title sequence and you’ve got the fanfare for Selznick and stuff like that, and I’ve used up about twelve ideas in the first 45 seconds! There’s just so much going on, all these themes that you’d be revisiting later in the film, you’re setting up in the opening suites. Oh my God. I knew this, to some degree, but when you’re trying to mimic it with your material, to some degree, it’s like, “My God, this score is doing such a lot of work and we haven’t even got into the film yet!” It’s also incredibly seamless: it didn’t feel like you were being bombarded. It was seamless the way it would flow from one melodic idea to another.</p>\n<p class=\"p1\">I come from a rock-and-roll background, pretty punk rock, like, “Fuck you, this is the way I’m doing it.” But obviously that doesn’t work all the time. That’s a nice attitude to have, that your stuff is edgy, that you can break barriers. But that sort of falls down when you’re actually telling stories. You still have to adhere to the rules – you’re there to support the picture and the storytelling, so you are following orders, to some degree. Yes, you can move it around and try [other things], but essentially, you’re there to support what’s going on on screen. I really did enjoy that old-fashioned, classical storytelling with the music, which I’ve kind of always tried to do and I hope I do that, but literary works for the screen bring an extra challenge. I really enjoyed it. I loved the book and the original film, so for me, to be able to write for Danvers, particularly, and the void that is Rebecca, was brilliant.</p>\n<p class=\"p1\"><b>There’s a sadness, almost a melancholy that permeates songs like <a href=\"https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=f5a1V6A7mPg\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener\">“Rebecca Always Rebecca.”</a> Can you talk about how you go about translating a specific emotion into a piece of music?</b></p>\n<p class=\"p1\">I think it’s partly to do with, inherently, one’s own personality. I don’t know that I’m a great actor in that respect. I think I can kind of channel things that speak to me, and I think that’s probably borne out by the work that I’ve done. It’s not like I suddenly pop up doing a comedy romp. There are certain things that I feel like I’m wired to do, and I probably look for those elements in the story. I’m not going to give any points of the story away, but we feel Rebecca obviously all the way through the film, and the second Mrs. de Winter’s view on her is – she’s being gaslit to some degree – but she doesn’t know the full story. So there’s the way she interprets what those feelings are that are around her, and of course one of those would be melancholy at what she perceives as Maxim’s loss and as Danvers’ loss, and even Manderley’s loss. The joyous spirit that she imagined was there in Rebecca’s time is now absent. So finding that was probably the thing I was looking forward to the most. It’s almost like a misdirection, but it’s also a truth to the second Mrs. de Winter at that time.</p>\n<p class=\"p1\"><b>I’ve heard about composers using unconventional items as instruments in some of their scores to help create sounds that are unique to that specific project. Was there anything like that for your work on <i>Rebecca</i>?</b></p>\n<p class=\"p1\">Not specifically, but with the demos, I did a lot of things on an instrument called a erhu, it’s like a two-string violin. It has Eastern connotations, which obviously didn’t really fit into the palette and gave it a vaguely mystical vibe in the demos, which wasn’t really what I was wanting. What I was wanting was a haunting droning effect, and we achieved that in the end with electric cello. It really brought it to life. Demos are weird and wonderful things: they sort of say everything and give you an idea of almost nothing you’re going to get when you record the [final score]. Maybe it’s just my demo making, I don’t know. But talking about the old days, Franz Waxman probably would have played his ideas to Hitchcock on the piano, he would never have done mocked up versions of stuff. It would have been, “Oh, this will be Manderley, this is Danvers, this is Maxim, this will be the court scene,” and stuff like that.</p>\n<p class=\"p1\">I’m very fortunate to record at Air Studios in London…a wonderful place to record. I’m an untrained musician. But I’ve never been anything but welcomed by trained musicians and professionals who bring my music to life, because they’re so excited by ideas and performance and playing. Those are the things you just can’t get in a demo…using things like the glass armonica, which is an amazing instrument that’s made by glass bowls and you play by wetting your fingers and pedaling and they revolve. They sound like wine glasses. But it’s an amazing instrument, and there are very few places in the world where you can go and get that sound.</p>\n<p class=\"p1\"><b>I’ve never heard of that instrument before. I’ll have to look that up.</b></p>\n<p class=\"p1\">Oh man, you’ve gotta check it out. It was invented by Benjamin Franklin, and it was called “the devil’s instrument” for the longest time, because you learned to play it, and within two years you’d be dead.</p>\n<p class=\"p1\"><b>What? (laughs incredulously)</b></p>\n<p class=\"p1\">It happened all the time. When you look at it, you’ll realize why. These glass bowls ascend in size, and they’re on a spindle, and you can pedal that and revolve, as if you were running your finger around a wine glass, but the wine glass itself was moving. It’s set up symphonically a little bit like a piano, but the black keys nowadays have gold leaf on them so you know which ones are black keys. But back in the day when they first did it, they used lead paint. So basically you’d be mainlining lead when you played it, and it’d drive you crazy and you’d die.</p>\n<p class=\"p1\"><b>Wow.</b></p>\n<p class=\"p1\">It was the most amazing thing. Yeah, it’s an incredible instrument. If you email me, I’ve got a little clip of <b>Alasdair Malloy</b>, who played it on <i>High-Rise</i>, that I can send you that maybe you can put on the website.</p>\n<p><iframe width=\"740\" height=\"416\" src=\"https://www.youtube.com/embed/JVqkY2cIz_w?feature=oembed\" frameborder=\"0\" allow=\"accelerometer; autoplay; clipboard-write; encrypted-media; gyroscope; picture-in-picture\" allowfullscreen></iframe></p>\n<p class=\"p1\"><b>Were there any specific moments discussed beforehand where you knew a particular track would need to do a lot of heavy lifting, or do you have to create the entire score with the mentality that it all needs to sort of be able to keep the movie afloat during any moments without dialogue?</b></p>\n<p class=\"p1\">Not really. Working with Ben, things sort of develop out of what we’ve got in front of us. With scenes like the dream sequences, they’re so sound-designed, too. I never felt like it was totally reliant on score, or that the score had to take the lead. It’s sort of a real choreographed event, where things worked in tandem. I love working with Ben, but his work evolves through what he’s presented with. Nothing is set in stone. It sort of naturally evolves to where it takes us, and I love that.</p>\n<p class=\"p1\"><b>What was the aspect of this score that you’re the most proud of?</b></p>\n<p class=\"p1\">(laughs) Well, to be honest, it’s probably the same as every score: that we got it done. Obviously I’ve been doing this for twenty years or whatever it is now, but it still feels like every time you start with a blank page, you go, “Oh my God, what am I going to do? Why do I even start?” It really does seem like such a mountain to climb. Yes, you’ve got inspiration in front of you, the work of the book, the director, the actors, the atmosphere, the sound effects, the look, the cinematography – everything to feed off of and join in with something that will hopefully really work and excite people. But the first day on the job, it’s just like, “Oh my God, what if this is the one that doesn’t get done?”</p>\n<p class=\"p1\">It’s a bit like, not that I have any children, but I can only compare it to having children – which may be doing a great disservice to all the women who have had babies and the men who have lived through the experience. I really don’t know. But for me, it is sort of like birthing something and you kind of go through a lot of turmoil, a lot of anxiety, a lot of joy, and in time, you remember nothing about it once it’s actually done because you’re sort of overwhelmed by the creation of it. So the real joy of actually having it be done and you’re listening to the final mixes, you see the final film and go, “There it is.” It’s like memories: you remember good things, mostly. Yes, you can sort of dip into the occasional bad memory, but you spend to not spend too much time with them. So the result is always the thing that I’m most proud of, that we actually did it.</p>\n<p class=\"p1\"><b>Well, this result was fantastic and everything came together really beautifully. I love the score. Thanks for taking the time.</b></p>\n<p class=\"p1\">Thank you very much, I appreciate that.</p>\n<p style=\"text-align: center;\">***</p>\n<p><em>Rebecca</em> arrives on Netflix on <strong>October 21</strong>, <strong>2020</strong>. You can read our review of the film <a href=\"https://www.slashfilm.com/rebecca-review/\">here</a>.</p>\n<p>The post <a rel=\"nofollow\" href=\"https://www.slashfilm.com/clint-mansell-on-composing-the-rebecca-score-in-the-shadow-of-hitchcock-incorporating-the-devils-instrument-and-more-interview/\">Clint Mansell on Composing the &#8216;Rebecca&#8217; Score in the Shadow of Hitchcock, Incorporating &#8220;The Devil&#8217;s Instrument,&#8221; and More [Interview]</a> appeared first on <a rel=\"nofollow\" 00:44:33","updatedTime":"2020-10-17 00:44:33","title":"‘Ammonite’ Trailer: Kate Winslet and Saoirse Ronan Are Star-Crossed Lovers","link":"https://www.slashfilm.com/?p=641488","description":"<p><img class=\"aligncenter size-medium wp-image-641496\" src=\"https://www.slashfilm.com/wp/wp-content/images/ammonite-e1602776766774-700x345.jpg\" alt=\"ammonite trailer\" width=\"700\" height=\"345\" srcset=\"https://www.slashfilm.com/wp/wp-content/images/ammonite-e1602776766774-700x345.jpg 700w, https://www.slashfilm.com/wp/wp-content/images/ammonite-e1602776766774-360x177.jpg 360w, https://www.slashfilm.com/wp/wp-content/images/ammonite-e1602776766774-768x378.jpg 768w, https://www.slashfilm.com/wp/wp-content/images/ammonite-e1602776766774.jpg 1439w\" sizes=\"(max-width: 700px) 100vw, 700px\" /></p>\n<p>Ahead of the its premiere in the U.K. at the close of the BFI London Film Festival, Lionsgate U.K. has debuted a new trailer for <strong><em>Ammonite</em></strong>. The windswept lesbian romance film stars <strong>Kate Winslet</strong> and <strong>Saoirse Ronan</strong> as a paleontologist and a miserable housewife who strike up a star-crossed romance for the ages. Watch the <em>Ammonite</em> trailer.</p>\n<p><span id=\"more-641488\"></span></p>\n<h2>Ammonite Trailer</h2>\n<p><iframe width=\"740\" height=\"416\" src=\"https://www.youtube.com/embed/MpHa6Fi75ck?feature=oembed\" frameborder=\"0\" allow=\"accelerometer; autoplay; clipboard-write; encrypted-media; gyroscope; picture-in-picture\" allowfullscreen></iframe></p>\n<p>Written and directed by Francis Lee, <em>Ammonite</em> is the stuff of awards contender dreams: an LGBT period drama romance headlined by an Oscar winner and Oscar nominee. And indeed, who wouldn&#8217;t want to see Kate Winslet and Saoirse Ronan romance each other in what appears to be a British spin on last year&#8217;s sublime <em>Portrait of a Lady on Fire</em>?</p>\n<p>It&#8217;s a concept that has won over many critics since <em>Ammonite</em> made its world premiere at the Toronto International Film Festival in September, and wins over a fair few more as the movie makes its festival rounds. But it has earned several negative reviews, including one from /Film&#8217;s Jason Gorber at TIFF, who <a href=\"https://www.slashfilm.com/ammonite-review/\">wrote</a>, &#8220;Like the fossils encrusted in rock there’s a core aspect of this film that’s certainly worth digging out, it’s just not entirely excavated through its execution, still trapped in its austere and distanced style. Lee’s film lacks the precision of a <i>Barry Lyndon</i> or the exquisitely intense gaze of <i>Portrait of a Lady on Fire</i>, making <i>Ammonite</i>’s deliberate pace and chilly characters feel little more than trinkets in a sea-side store – fine to look at, but little more.&#8221;</p>\n<p>I still remain intrigued by <em>Ammonite</em> despite the divisive reviews, though it&#8217;s uncertain when I&#8217;ll get a chance to decide for myself, as the trailer only teases that the film is &#8220;coming soon.&#8221; No release date has yet been set for either theaters or VOD.</p>\n<p>Here is the synopsis for <em>Ammonite</em>, which also stars <strong>Fiona Shaw</strong>, <strong>Gemma Jones</strong>, <strong>Alec Secareanu</strong>, and <strong>James McArdle</strong>.:</p>\n<blockquote><p>In the 1840s, acclaimed self-taught palaeontologist Mary Anning works alone on the wild and brutal Southern English coastline of Lyme Regis. The days of her famed discoveries behind her, she now hunts for common fossils to sell to rich tourists to support herself and her ailing widowed mother. When one such tourist, Roderick Murchison, arrives in Lyme on the first leg of a European tour, he entrusts Mary with the care of his young wife Charlotte, who is recuperating from a personal tragedy. Mary, whose life is a daily struggle on the poverty line, cannot afford to turn him down but, proud and relentlessly passionate about her work, she clashes with her unwanted guest. They are two women from utterly different worlds. Yet despite the chasm between their social spheres and personalities, Mary and Charlotte discover they can each offer what the other has been searching for: the realisation that they are not alone. It is the beginning of a passionate and all-consuming love affair that will defy all social bounds and alter the course of both lives irrevocably.</p></blockquote>\n<p>The post <a rel=\"nofollow\" href=\"https://www.slashfilm.com/ammonite-trailer-official/\">&#8216;Ammonite&#8217; Trailer: Kate Winslet and Saoirse Ronan Are Star-Crossed Lovers</a> appeared first on <a rel=\"nofollow\" href=\"https://www.slashfilm.com\">/Film</a>.</p>\n<div class=\"feedflare\">\n<a href=\"http://feeds.feedburner.com/~ff/slashfilm?a=Mpawe8vkxmA:ZOMx5W5dzv4:yIl2AUoC8zA\"><img 00:44:33","updatedTime":"2020-10-17 00:44:33","title":"Lupita Nyong’o and Danai Gurira’s ‘Americanah’ Series Not Moving Forward at HBO Max Due to COVID-19","link":"https://www.slashfilm.com/?p=641535","description":"<p><img class=\"aligncenter wp-image-641539 size-medium\" src=\"https://www.slashfilm.com/wp/wp-content/images/danai-gurira-lupita-nyongo-e1602787309939-700x335.jpeg\" alt=\"americanah tv series\" width=\"700\" height=\"335\" srcset=\"https://www.slashfilm.com/wp/wp-content/images/danai-gurira-lupita-nyongo-e1602787309939-700x335.jpeg 700w, https://www.slashfilm.com/wp/wp-content/images/danai-gurira-lupita-nyongo-e1602787309939-360x173.jpeg 360w, https://www.slashfilm.com/wp/wp-content/images/danai-gurira-lupita-nyongo-e1602787309939.jpeg 768w\" sizes=\"(max-width: 700px) 100vw, 700px\" /></p>\n<p><strong><em>Americanah</em></strong> joins the growing list of shows that have been axed due to the coronavirus (COVID-19) pandemic. <strong>Lupita Nyong&#8217;o</strong> and <strong>Danai Gurira</strong>&#8216;s TV series adaptation of the <strong>Chimamanda Ngozi Adichie</strong> novel is no longer moving forward at HBO Max after COVID-19 production delays led to scheduling conflicts for star and executive producer Nyong&#8217;o, who was forced to drop out of the series.</p>\n<p><span id=\"more-641535\"></span></p>\n<p><a href=\"https://variety.com/2020/tv/news/lupita-nyongo-danai-gurira-americanah-series-hbo-max-2-1234806145/\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener\">Variety</a> broke the news that the <em>Americanah </em>TV series is no longer moving forward at HBO Max after Nyong&#8217;o was forced to exit the series due to scheduling conflicts brought on by COVID-19 delays.</p>\n<p>The series had originally received a <a href=\"https://variety.com/2019/tv/news/lupita-nyongo-danai-gurira-americanah-series-hbo-max-1203335299/\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener\">10-episode straight-to-series order</a> by HBO Max as part of its growing Max Original line-up — one of the streamer&#8217;s many high profile projects buoyed by <em>Black Panther </em>co-stars Nyong&#8217;o and Gurira&#8217;s involvement as executive producers. Nyong&#8217;o was set to star while Gurira was set to write and serve as showrunner. Along with Nyong&#8217;o, the series was set to star Uzo Aduba, Corey Hawkins, Zackary Momoh, and Tireni Oyenusi.</p>\n<p>Here was the logline for <em>Americanah</em>:</p>\n<blockquote><p>It tells the story of Ifemelu (Nyong’o), a young, beautiful, self-assured woman raised in Nigeria, who as a teenager falls in love with her classmate Obinze (Momoh). Living in a military-ruled country, they each depart for the west, with Ifemelu heading for America, where, despite her academic success, she is forced to grapple for the first time with what it means to be black. Quiet, thoughtful Obinze had hoped to join her, but with post-9/11 America closed to him, he instead plunges into a dangerous undocumented life in London.</p></blockquote>\n<p><em>Americanah </em>was a longtime passion project for Nyong&#8217;o, who had previously planned to adapt Adichie&#8217;s novel into a film in which she would star alongside David Oyelowo. The project turned into a series in 2018, and was picked up by HBO Max last year. Things were going swimmingly for the project, with shooting planned to begin in early 2020, until the coronavirus pandemic hit. Production dates were pushed back until it was untenable for Nyong&#8217;o to stay on board due to conflicts with other projects on her schedule.</p>\n<p><em>Americanah</em> is just the latest casualty of the pandemic. Netflix has canned shows like <em>The Society, I Am Not Okay With This</em>, and <i>GLOW</i>. Showtime also canceled the acclaimed Kirsten Dunst-led <em>On Becoming a God in Central Florida</em> just before its second season. While shows — both beloved and less so — around the board have been getting the axe, curiously, <a href=\"https://ew.com/tv/all-the-shows-unrenewed-covid-19/\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener\">a majority</a> of the canceled series have been led by women or people of color. While <em>Americanah</em> is a case of an unavailable star, its cancelation unfortunately does add to that pattern.</p>\n<p>The post <a rel=\"nofollow\" href=\"https://www.slashfilm.com/americanah-tv-series-canceled/\">Lupita Nyong&#8217;o and Danai Gurira&#8217;s &#8216;Americanah&#8217; Series Not Moving Forward at HBO Max Due to COVID-19</a> appeared first on <a rel=\"nofollow\" href=\"https://www.slashfilm.com\">/Film</a>.</p>\n<div class=\"feedflare\">\n<a 00:44:33","updatedTime":"2020-10-17 00:44:33","title":"‘Buck Rogers’ Movie in the Works at Legendary, Could Spawn Television and Anime Spin-Offs","link":"https://www.slashfilm.com/?p=641469","description":"<p><a href=\"https://www.slashfilm.com/wp/wp-content/images/Buck-Rogers-movie.jpg\"><img class=\"aligncenter size-medium wp-image-641491\" src=\"https://www.slashfilm.com/wp/wp-content/images/Buck-Rogers-movie-700x321.jpg\" alt=\"Buck Rogers movie\" width=\"700\" height=\"321\" srcset=\"https://www.slashfilm.com/wp/wp-content/images/Buck-Rogers-movie-700x321.jpg 700w, https://www.slashfilm.com/wp/wp-content/images/Buck-Rogers-movie-360x165.jpg 360w, https://www.slashfilm.com/wp/wp-content/images/Buck-Rogers-movie-768x352.jpg 768w, https://www.slashfilm.com/wp/wp-content/images/Buck-Rogers-movie.jpg 948w\" sizes=\"(max-width: 700px) 100vw, 700px\" /></a></p>\n<p>Hollywood is once again trying to bring space opera hero<strong> <em>Buck Rogers</em></strong> to the big screen. Legendary Pictures and producers <strong>Don Murphy</strong> and <strong>Susan Montford</strong> are developing a new <em>Buck Rogers</em> movie with hopes of launching a franchise – because these days you can&#8217;t adapt a character like this <em>without</em> promising it&#8217;ll spawn several sequels. The character originated in the pulp magazine <i>Amazing Stories </i>in 1928 and went on to appear in a comic strip, a radio program, a film serial, a television series, and more. In the stories, Buck Rogers is a man from the 20th century who is knocked out in an abandoned mine, only to wake up in the year 2429 A.D.</p>\n<p><span id=\"more-641469\"></span></p>\n<p><a href=\"https://www.hollywoodreporter.com/news/buck-rogers-movie-in-the-works-at-legendary\">THR</a> is reporting that a new <em>Buck Rogers</em> movie is in the works over at Legendary. But Legendary doesn&#8217;t want to just stop at a film – oh, no. Instead, they&#8217;re hoping that the big screen take will launch &#8220;a prestige television series as well as an anime series, giving audiences a 360-look at heroics sets in the 25th century.&#8221; Legendary is taking a similar approach with their upcoming sci-fi epic <em>Dune</em>, which is set to spin-off into a TV series (and probably more), as well as sequels – provided it ends up making enough money at the box office.</p>\n<p>As a character, <em>Buck Rogers</em> has been around since 1928, and has appeared in all sorts of media, from comics, to radio dramas, to TV shows, and beyond. There was a 1979 film called <em>Buck Rogers in the 25th Century</em>, which was actually a pilot for a new TV series. Rather than release the pilot on TV, Universal opted to release it in theaters before the series, also called <em>Buck Rogers in the 25th</em> <em>Century</em>, premiered.</p>\n<p>Over the years, several filmmakers have attempted to get a new <em>Buck Rogers</em> movie off the ground. At one point, comic book writer and artist, and sometimes director <a href=\"https://www.slashfilm.com/frank-miller-to-direct-buck-rogers-2/\">Frank Miller</a> was hoping to make his own <em>Buck Rogers</em> flick. That never happened, probably because everyone watched Miller&#8217;s <em>The Spirit</em> movie and realized he should never, ever be allowed to make a film again.</p>\n<p>After Miller, <a href=\"https://www.slashfilm.com/paul-ws-anderson-directing-buck-rogers-3d/\">Paul WS Anderson</a>, of <em>Resident Evil</em> fame, was attached to direct a 3D <em>Buck Rogers</em> film. This was in 2010, a year after <em>Avatar</em>, when Hollywood suddenly thought every big budget movie <em>needed</em> to be in 3D. Like the Miller movie, the Anderson <em>Buck Rogers</em> never got off the ground. There&#8217;s no filmmaker attached to this latest attempt just yet, but stay tuned.</p>\n<p>The post <a rel=\"nofollow\" href=\"https://www.slashfilm.com/buck-rogers-movie/\">&#8216;Buck Rogers&#8217; Movie in the Works at Legendary, Could Spawn Television and Anime Spin-Offs</a> appeared first on <a rel=\"nofollow\" href=\"https://www.slashfilm.com\">/Film</a>.</p>\n<div 18:30:50 00:44:33","updatedTime":"2020-10-17 00:44:33","title":"Luke Skywalker Died in George Lucas’ Original Treatment For ‘Star Wars: Episode 8’","link":"https://www.slashfilm.com/?p=641248","description":"<p><img class=\"alignnone size-medium wp-image-459599 aligncenter\" src=\"https://www.slashfilm.com/wp/wp-content/images/starwars-lastjedi-luke-lightsaber-finalbattle3-700x292.jpg\" alt=\"Luke Skywalker died\" width=\"700\" height=\"292\" srcset=\"https://www.slashfilm.com/wp/wp-content/images/starwars-lastjedi-luke-lightsaber-finalbattle3-700x292.jpg 700w, https://www.slashfilm.com/wp/wp-content/images/starwars-lastjedi-luke-lightsaber-finalbattle3-360x150.jpg 360w, https://www.slashfilm.com/wp/wp-content/images/starwars-lastjedi-luke-lightsaber-finalbattle3.jpg 1430w\" sizes=\"(max-width: 700px) 100vw, 700px\" /></p>\n<p>Details about <strong>George Lucas</strong>&#8216;s original unused treatments for the <em><strong>Star Wars</strong> </em>sequel trilogy have been somewhat hard to come by over the years. But thanks to a new book written by Lucasfilm&#8217;s <strong>Pablo Hidalgo</strong>, we know Lucas&#8217; plans for the new trilogy share at least two major things in common with the versions that ultimately hit screens: Luke Skywalker died in Lucas&#8217;s treatment for <em>Episode 8</em>, and the idea for the character of Rey was there from the start (although she had a different name when Lucas was envisioning her). Read on to get the details.<span id=\"more-641248\"></span></p>\n<p>In Hidalgo&#8217;s new book <strong><em>Star Wars: Fascinating Facts: Story, Lore &#38; History From The Greatest Galaxy</em></strong>, the author reveals that &#8220;years before <em>The Last Jedi</em> began development, the treatment left behind by George Lucas in 2012 also had <em>Episode 8</em> be the one wherein Luke Skywalker would die.&#8221;</p>\n<p>That is fascinating for multiple reasons. Though Luke Skywalker died in Rian Johnson&#8217;s <em>Star Wars: The Last Jedi</em>, the iconic character&#8217;s death wasn&#8217;t the main sticking point for the vocal contingent of fans who didn&#8217;t like that film – many of them didn&#8217;t appreciate the fundamental type of character that Luke evolved into in that film. However, the book notes that Lucas&#8217; plans for Luke were very similar to what Johnson ultimately delivered. In his treatments, Luke has become &#8220;a recluse, withdrawn into a very dark space and needs to be drawn back from despair.&#8221; So while <em>The Last Jedi</em> remains controversial, it seems like Lucas and Johnson were very much on the same page.</p>\n<p>Ultimately, it&#8217;s an interesting coincidence that if George Lucas had his way, he would have also killed Luke in that same movie instead of waiting until the final installment in the nine-movie Skywalker saga. It&#8217;s especially interesting because it presents a conflicting narrative to one that actor Mark Hamill has previously put forth in the press. Hamill has gone on record saying that Lucas&#8217;s original plan did not, in fact, feature Luke dying in <em>Episode 8</em>, but instead held his death until <em>Episode 9</em>.</p>\n<p>&#8220;I happen to know that George didn&#8217;t kill Luke until the end of [<em>Episode</em>] <em>9</em>, after he trained Leia, which is another thread that was never played upon [in <em>The Last Jedi</em>],&#8221; Hamill <a href=\"https://www.slashfilm.com/episode-9-ending/\">said in 2018</a>. So&#8230;who is correct here?</p>\n<p>Also, we previously knew that before landing on the character name of &#8220;Rey,&#8221; J.J. Abrams referred to Daisy Ridley&#8217;s character as &#8220;Kira,&#8221; a name which lasted through much of <em>The Force Awakens</em>&#8216; pre-production. But this book claims that &#8220;one idea that remained constant from the start was that of a young woman&#8217;s quest to become a Jedi Knight. In George Lucas&#8217;s original outline, she was a 14-year-old girl named Taryn. In his subsequent iterations, she would briefly be named Thea and – believe it or not – Winkie.&#8221;</p>\n<p>Winkie, huh? If that sounds unbelievable&#8230;well, we thought so too. And that&#8217;s why we ordered a copy of this new book and independently confirmed all of this after this news started circulating around the internet earlier this week. Yep, it&#8217;s all in there and it&#8217;s real.</p>\n<p>Ultimately, all of this leaves us wondering just how much of Lucas&#8217;s original treatments made their way into the final versions of the sequel trilogy. We know that his plans <a href=\"https://www.slashfilm.com/george-lucas-star-wars-sequel-trilogy-plans/\">included the Whills and the return of midi-chlorians</a>, but perhaps there wasn&#8217;t quite as much thrown out as we once assumed.</p>\n<p>The post <a rel=\"nofollow\" href=\"https://www.slashfilm.com/luke-skywalker-died-in-george-lucas-star-wars-8/\">Luke Skywalker Died in George Lucas&#8217; Original Treatment For &#8216;Star Wars: Episode 8&#8217;</a> appeared first on <a rel=\"nofollow\" href=\"https://www.slashfilm.com\">/Film</a>.</p>\n<div class=\"feedflare\">\n<a Dreams","link":"https://www.slashfilm.com/?p=641578","description":"<p><a href=\"https://www.slashfilm.com/wp/wp-content/images/penny-dreadful-the-quarantine-stream.jpg\"><img class=\"aligncenter href=\"https://slashfilm.com/tag/the-quarantine-stream\"><strong>The Quarantine Stream</strong></a>, a new series where the /Film team shares what they’ve been watching while social distancing during the COVID-19 pandemic.)</em></p>\n<p><strong>The Show: </strong><em>Penny Dreadful</em></p>\n<p><strong>Where You Can Stream It: </strong>Netflix</p>\n<p><strong>The Pitch: </strong>Gothic horrors from classic literature collide in Victorian England.</p>\n<p><strong>Why It&#8217;s Essential Quarantine Viewing: </strong>Gory, sexy, and scary, <em>Penny Dreadful</em> is a show that deserved better. While the terrible <em>American Horror Story</em> gets to stretch on into infinity, this better-made, better-written, better-acted series was mostly ignored by a wider audience and only lasted three seasons (although it did get a recent reboot, which I haven&#8217;t seen). But those craving all sorts of gothic goodness – tales of vampires, werewolves, immortals, and more – will find plenty to love here. It&#8217;s the ultimate Halloween season show.</p>\n<p><span id=\"more-641578\"></span></p>\n<p>Pulling from <em>Frankenstein</em>, <em>Dracula</em>, <em>Dr. Jekyll and</em><em> Mr. Hyde</em>, <em>The Picture of Dorian Gray</em>, and more, <em>Penny Dreadful </em>is a loving tribute to gothic horror. Set in foggy, filthy Victorian England, the show follows a team of rag-tag loners and misfits who come together to fight evil forces. At the center of the show is <strong>Eva Green</strong> as Vanessa Ives, a heroine with a dark past. Green&#8217;s work on the show is phenomenal, and part of the reason I&#8217;ve never bothered to check out <em>Penny Dreadful: City of Angels</em> is that I can&#8217;t imagine watching any form of the series without her.</p>\n<p>Then there&#8217;s <strong>Reeve Carney</strong> as the immortal Dorian Gray;<strong> Timothy Dalton</strong> as noble hunter Malcolm Murray; <strong>Rory Kinnear</strong> as the Frankenstein Monster; <strong>Billie Piper</strong> as a kind of Bride of Frankenstein (she dies of consumption and then gets brought back to life); <strong>Harry Treadaway</strong> as Dr. Frankenstein; and<strong> Josh Hartnett</strong> as Ethan Chandler, the lone American in the group who <em>also</em> happens to be a werewolf.</p>\n<p>The &#8220;dark characters from classic Brit lit who team up&#8221; idea isn&#8217;t new – Alan Moore made it famous with his <em>League of Extraordinary Gentlemen</em> comic, after all. But what <em>Penny Dreadful</em> lacks in originality it makes up for in style. It&#8217;s a show full of crumbling castles, dark alleys, and haunted houses. Seances go terrible wrong; blood rains down on waltzers during a party; fog wafts through every street; and vampires, witches, and other creatures of the night lurk around every corner.</p>\n<p>There&#8217;s a romance that blooms between Vanessa and Ethan, and it&#8217;s sweet, and tender, and, of course, doomed. All of these characters are doomed. But that doesn&#8217;t make the series any less entertaining. Best of all, writer and creator John Logan and company play with expectations. Yes, the characters in this show are from stories that everyone knows by heart by now. But <em>Penny Dreadful</em> doesn&#8217;t stick to the source material. Things we expect to happen don&#8217;t, leading to big, wicked surprises.</p>\n<p>Logan was made aware that the series was going to be canceled, and as a result, <em>Penny Dreadful</em> doesn&#8217;t end on a cliffhanger. It wraps its story up in season 3, although the way it goes about wrapping things up left several fans very upset (I&#8217;m somewhere in the middle; I don&#8217;t <em>love</em> the ending, but I think it <em>mostly</em> works, too).</p>\n<p>If you crave things that go bump in the night, and you <em>also</em> want most of those things that go bump in the night to be kind of sexy, you can&#8217;t go wrong with <em>Penny Dreadful</em>.</p>\n<p>The post <a rel=\"nofollow\" href=\"https://www.slashfilm.com/penny-dreadful-the-quarantine-stream/\">The Quarantine Stream: &#8216;Penny Dreadful&#8217; is the Beautiful, Lurid Gothic Horror Show of Your Dreams</a> appeared first on <a rel=\"nofollow\" href=\"https://www.slashfilm.com\">/Film</a>.</p>\n<div class=\"feedflare\">\n<a 00:44:33","updatedTime":"2020-10-17 00:44:33","title":"‘Game of Thrones’ Prequel Series ‘House of the Dragon’ Moves Away From Northern Ireland, Will Film in England Instead","link":"https://www.slashfilm.com/?p=641545","description":"<p><img class=\"alignnone size-medium wp-image-428745 aligncenter\" 360w\" sizes=\"(max-width: 700px) 100vw, 700px\" /></p>\n<p>It&#8217;s the end of an era.</p>\n<p><em>Game of Thrones</em> used the city of Belfast in Northern Ireland as its home base for all eight seasons of its run on HBO, and the expectation was that any prequel or spin-off shows would continue to shoot there. (Another prequel did film there, but that show wasn&#8217;t picked up.) But now Westeros will be created in another country altogether: according to a new report, the upcoming prequel series <em><strong>House of the Dragon</strong></em> will shift its home base away from Northern Ireland over to England, just northwest of London.<span id=\"more-641545\"></span></p>\n<p><em>Game of Thrones</em> fan site <a href=\"https://winteriscoming.net/2020/10/14/house-of-the-dragon-production-headquarters-game-of-thrones-prequel/\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener\">Winter is Coming</a> relays the news that the <em>House of the Dragon</em> filming locations will primarily be in Leavesden Studios in Watford, England. The facility has hosted productions like <em>GoldenEye</em>, <em>Star Wars: Episode I – The Phantom Menace</em>, every <em>Harry Potter</em> film, <em>The Dark Knight</em>, <em>Inception</em>, <em>Edge of Tomorrow</em>, <em>Paddington</em>, <em>Wonder Woman 1984</em>, and <em>F9</em>, just to name a few. It&#8217;s currently home to Matt Reeves&#8217; <em>The Batman</em>. I <a href=\"https://www.slashfilm.com/spider-man-far-from-home-set-visit/\">visited the studio</a> while <em>Spider-Man: Far From Home</em> was shooting there, and it is absolutely massive: the huge water tank, several sound stages, and its gigantic backlot area barely scratch the surface of everything it has to offer.</p>\n<p>The studio is owned by Warner Bros., which is in the same corporate family as HBO, so the thinking may have been to keep <em>House of the Dragon</em> under a familiar roof. (It also could be a way for execs to keep a closer eye on this production and a tighter hand on the reins, with this base being just outside of London.)</p>\n<p>Of course, this is a massive blow to the people of Northern Ireland. When I <a href=\"https://www.slashfilm.com/game-of-thrones-locations/\">visited the original Winterfell</a> a couple of years ago, it was clear that <em>Game of Thrones</em> was a major source of pride for the people there and the tourism from its global fanbase was pumping tons of money into its economy. (Whether or not the show&#8217;s divisive ending will stem the flow of visitors remains to be seen.)</p>\n<p>Rumor has it that <em>House of the Dragon</em> will ramp up in January 2021 and land on HBO sometime in <strong>2022</strong>. The show, which takes place <a href=\"https://www.slashfilm.com/house-of-the-dragon-series/\">300 years before the events</a> of <em>Game of Thrones</em>, has already <a href=\"https://www.slashfilm.com/game-of-thrones-prequel-cast-paddy-considine/\">cast</a> <strong>Paddy Considine</strong> to play King Viserys Targaryen. It&#8217;s set largely at Dragonstone, which was created in <em>Game of Thrones</em> using a combination of locations in Northern Ireland and Spain, so it&#8217;ll be interesting to compare and contrast once we get our first look at the new (well, chronologically older) version.</p>\n<p>The post <a rel=\"nofollow\" href=\"https://www.slashfilm.com/house-of-the-dragon-filming-locations/\">&#8216;Game of Thrones&#8217; Prequel Series &#8216;House of the Dragon&#8217; Moves Away From Northern Ireland, Will Film in Album","link":"https://www.slashfilm.com/?p=641512","description":"<p><img class=\"alignnone size-medium wp-image-641532 aligncenter\" src=\"https://www.slashfilm.com/wp/wp-content/images/Bruce-Springsteens-Letter-to-You-Trailer-700x300.jpg\" alt=\"Bruce Springsteen's Letter to You Trailer\" width=\"700\" height=\"300\" srcset=\"https://www.slashfilm.com/wp/wp-content/images/Bruce-Springsteens-Letter-to-You-Trailer.jpg 700w, https://www.slashfilm.com/wp/wp-content/images/Bruce-Springsteens-Letter-to-You-Trailer-360x154.jpg 360w\" sizes=\"(max-width: 700px) 100vw, 700px\" /></p>\n<p>Rock and roll icons <strong>Bruce Springsteen</strong> and The E Street Band haven&#8217;t recorded an album together live since 1984&#8217;s <em>Born in the U.S.A.</em>, but more than 35 years later, they headed back in the studio to do it again – and a camera crew was present to capture the action. The resulting footage has been edited into a documentary called <em><strong>Bruce Springsteen&#8217;s Letter to You</strong></em>, which hits AppleTV+ in just a few days. Check out the trailer below, which showcases the ever-evolving relationship between The Boss and his band and the sometimes chaotic environment in which they recorded their latest songs.<span id=\"more-641512\"></span></p>\n<h2>Bruce Springsteen&#8217;s Letter to You Trailer</h2>\n<p><iframe width=\"740\" height=\"416\" src=\"https://www.youtube.com/embed/LuVRWA7r_dU?feature=oembed\" frameborder=\"0\" allow=\"accelerometer; autoplay; clipboard-write; encrypted-media; gyroscope; picture-in-picture\" allowfullscreen></iframe></p>\n<p>Springsteen, who has won 20 Grammys, an Oscar, a Tony, and two Golden Globe Awards over his impressive five-decade career, is arguably the king of the heartland rock subgenre. He&#8217;s a prolific songwriter who has 19 albums (and tons of unreleased material) under his belt already, and in recent years, he&#8217;s become known for his lengthy concert performances. While many bands (in the pre-COVID era) would come out and play for 90 minutes and then pack it up, Springsteen would regularly blast past that, sometimes even playing shows that were four hours long.</p>\n<p>His newest album, <em>Letter to You</em>, is coming out later this month, and coinciding with that release is the premiere of this documentary, chronicling the mostly harmonious (but sometimes chaotic) making of the album. <strong>Thom Zimny</strong>, who has been making music videos for Springsteen and documenting the musician&#8217;s career for the past fifteen years (and has also taken detours into the lives of Elvis Presley, Michael Jackson, and Johnny Cash), directed this documentary. His most recent directing credit was <a href=\"https://www.slashfilm.com/western-stars-trailer/\"><em>Western Stars</em></a>, the 2019 live concert performance doc about Springsteen.</p>\n<p>It&#8217;s a good time to be a Springsteen fan right now, what with his recent Broadway show being captured as a <a href=\"https://www.slashfilm.com/springsteen-on-broadway-review/\">Netflix special</a>, his music inspiring the recent movie <a href=\"https://www.slashfilm.com/blinded-by-the-light-trailer/\"><em>Blinded by the Light</em></a>, and now this. Here is the documentary&#8217;s official description:</p>\n<blockquote><p>Go behind the music as Bruce Springsteen and The E Street Band record together live for the first time since Born In The U.S.A. in this intimate documentary that captures reflections on love, loss, and the way music has shaped Bruce Springsteen&#8217;s life.</p></blockquote>\n<p><em>Bruce Springsteen&#8217;s Letter to You</em> arrives on AppleTV+ on <strong>October 23</strong>, <strong>2020</strong>. Check out the music videos for the album&#8217;s first two singles, &#8220;Letter to You&#8221; and &#8220;Ghosts,&#8221; below:</p>\n<p><iframe width=\"740\" height=\"416\" Fantasy","link":"https://www.slashfilm.com/?p=641570","description":"<p><img class=\"alignnone size-medium wp-image-641573 aligncenter\"
